core.classifier.bisac module¶

class core.classifier.bisac.BISACClassifier[source]¶

Bases: core.classifier.Classifier

Handle real, genuine, according-to-Hoyle BISAC classifications.

Subclasses of this method can use the same basic classification logic to classify classifications that are based on BISAC but have cosmetic differences.

First, a BISAC code is mapped to its human-readable name.

Second, the name is split into parts (e.g. [“Fiction”, “War & Military”]).

To determine fiction status, audience, target age, or genre, the list of name parts is compared against each of a list of matching rules.

core.classifier.keyword module¶

class core.classifier.keyword.Eg(term)[source]¶

Bases: object

Mark this string as an example of a classification, rather than an exact identifier for that classification. For example, basketball is an an example of a sport, but athletics is an identifier for the sports classification.

class core.classifier.keyword.LCSHClassifier[source]¶

class core.classifier.keyword.TAGClassifier[source]¶

Bases: core.classifier.keyword.KeywordBasedClassifier

core.classifier.keyword.match_kw(*l)[source]¶

Turn a list of strings into a function which uses a regular expression to match any of those strings, so long as there’s a word boundary on both ends. The function will match all the strings by default, or can exclude the strings that are examples of the classification.

core.classifier.rbdigital module¶

class core.classifier.rbdigital.RBDigitalAudienceClassifier[source]¶

Bases: core.classifier.FreeformAudienceClassifier

classmethod target_age(identifier, name)[source]¶

This tag might contain a grade level, an age in years, or nothing. We will try both a grade level and an age in years, but we will require that the tag indicate what’s being measured. A tag like “9-12” will not match anything because we don’t know if it’s age 9-12 or grade 9-12.

class core.classifier.rbdigital.RBDigitalSubjectClassifier[source]¶

Bases: core.classifier.keyword.KeywordBasedClassifier

classmethod audience(identifier, name)[source]¶

The two subjects here that imply an audience, juvenile-fiction and juvenile-nonfiction, could be either Childrens or YA, and RBdigital always provides a more specific audience classification, so we don’t try to derive audience information from an RBDigital Subject.

classmethod default_audience_for_target_age(range)[source]¶
classmethod default_target_age_for_audience(audience)[source]¶

The default target age for a given audience.

We don’t know what age range a children’s book is appropriate for, but we can make a decent guess for a YA book, for an ‘Adult’ book it’s pretty clear, and for an ‘Adults Only’ book it’s very clear.

add(classification)[source]¶

Prepare a single Classification for consideration.

audience(genres=[], default_audience=None)[source]¶

What’s the most likely audience for this book? :param default_audience: To avoid embarassing situations we will classify works as being intended for adults absent convincing evidence to the contrary. In some situations (like the metadata wrangler), it’s better to state that we have no information, so default_audience can be set to None.

audience_imprints = {'Harlequin Teen': 'Young Adult', 'HarperTeen': 'Young Adult', 'Open Road Media Teen & Tween': 'Young Adult', 'Rosen Young Adult': 'Young Adult'}¶
classify(default_fiction=None, default_audience=None)[source]¶
classmethod consolidate_genre_weights(weights, subgenre_swallows_parent_at=0.03)[source]¶

If a genre and its subgenres both show up, examine the subgenre with the highest weight. If its weight exceeds a certain proportion of the weight of the parent genre, assign the parent’s weight to the subgenre and remove the parent.

weigh_metadata()[source]¶

Modify the weights according to the given Work’s metadata.

Use work metadata to simulate classifications.

This is basic stuff, like: Harlequin tends to publish romances.
